How widely can these same approaches translate to other architectures of accelerator experiments and even more broadly beyond particle accelerator experiments?

[1:53:22] This idea of BAX or Bayesian Algorithm Execution is — if you know about Bayesian optimization you can think of this as a generalization of Bayesian optimization where you're separating out the thing that you're modeling from your objective function. And why I find that exciting is now you're able to have rather than having a Gaussian process with small number of points modeling a one-off objective function, you're actually using this now to really learn some physics. So your surrogate model is now an underlying physics model of your system.

[1:53:56] We've used this already for a wide range of topics, different types of accelerators, both control — that was originally what we were working on — but now increasingly working on design with it, and then also in other fields. So we also work in material science and people using it for drug design. So it's very general.

[1:54:11] The philosophical way to think about it is that we're working not just to find a point that optimizes some design or some task, but really to actually design this underlying model. And because now this underlying model can be a big neural network, that can be the outcome indeed of BAX. So we have a sort of loosely calling this idea of Foundation BAX — the idea of basically the surrogate model actually being a foundation model. So really thinking about this as a way of fine-tuning or training a new foundation model for the task that you care about.

[1:54:50] You can think about having a community using these tools to build their own foundation model. So in the materials world or in the future circular collider you have all of your design team all sharing, putting simulations into one central model that can support everyone. So in that sense I think actually it is really designed to be flexible and to transition to new applications.

[1:55:35] I was interested to see that the large hadron collider detector now — the triggering that actually records events when two protons collide — is now being run by AI. And one of the leaders of that implementation in the LHC is here with us today, Javier. Can you explain a little more about how the incredible low latency of this triggering algorithm — which I believe is less than one microsecond — has impacted science of the LHC and what are the remaining bottlenecks?

[1:56:34] The big challenge at the LHC is just — the rarity of the events that we're looking for means you need to collide a ton of protons in order to actually have any chance of seeing, you know, double Higgs boson production or something. So that means we are flooded with data. We collide protons 40 million times a second. So every 25 nanoseconds there's another collision and we can't possibly record and analyze all that data. There's just not enough compute budget in the world basically. So we have to down-select immediately, filter that down to something manageable.

[1:57:14] The first stage has to operate in about a microsecond. It's a big FPGA farm with some custom electronics, and has to reject something like 99.8% of the collisions right off the bat. If those precious new physics signals are there and you discard them in that step, you're never going to see it. So it is of really crucial importance.

[1:57:42] Traditionally we would do pretty simple algorithms because of how quick the latency was — basically just count up interesting particles that are rare, like muons, or measure the total amount of energy in the collision and then save it if it's above some threshold. What my work and the work of my collaborators has enabled is to replace that with much more multivariate neural network decisions that are based on not just one specific aspect of the event, but actually looking at the topology of the different particles in the event — how likely it is that one of the particles might be like a bottom quark, which Higgs bosons decay to. So it really has helped increase the acceptance to these rare signal events that we're looking for.

[1:58:30] Beyond that it's actually enabled a new thing that maybe was unexpected initially — we're interested not just in looking for what we know is there like the Higgs boson, we're interested in: is there anything unexpected in the data? So we designed these trigger algorithms based on the signatures that we know, but there might be some new physics that doesn't map onto our expectation. And so one of the exciting new things that this has enabled is doing unsupervised anomaly detection. Classically this is done with training an autoencoder or variational autoencoder and then using the reconstruction loss as a proxy for how anomalous an event is. If you train it on a bunch of standard model events and it basically can't compress a new event well, that could be a sign that it's new physics or something anomalous.

[1:59:23] That has been just recently deployed for the first time in the CMS experiment and ATLAS has done something similar. We now have a year or a few years of data recorded with this kind of trigger, so the next step is really analyzing that and seeing what comes out.

[1:59:42] There is still a bottleneck in terms of designing the best algorithm given the hardware constraints — this idea of co-design. We really want to automate that co-design loop where you're not just optimizing for performance of a machine learning algorithm but also simultaneously trying to make it as small and as fast as possible. We have new tools for this now relying on Bayesian optimization or genetic algorithms, and that's an exciting new direction.

[2:00:43] A lot of the things we did — we built this compiler framework HLS4ML for ML and it definitely has applications far beyond particle physics. People use it for neuroscience applications, material science. And this idea of co-design is really important: qubit readout, there are a lot of different applications. We have the fast machine learning for science conference coming up at UC San Diego at the end of August.

[2:01:18] There are many experiments that rely on reconstructing a science signal from a distributed network of many sensors. Some of your recent work for experimental direct dark matter detection has proposed a new type of AI-driven algorithm for reconstructing the science signal from the detector which is much more robust even in situations where traditional methods can suffer near 100% data loss. Could you explain first a bit more about how these advances work?

[2:02:16] To be clear the project where we are trying to integrate AI has not been completed yet. Regardless of AI or conventional processing, it's important to note that the type of work we are doing is very close to the sensors or detectors. It's not going to be after you stream out the data. The motivation is to try to solve the data problem right at the source. If we talk about distance, we are probably looking at micrometer up to maybe millimeter away of putting logic and transistors next to the detectors.

[2:03:02] One project I'm working on with Sean is to optimize the dark matter search. The setup is that we have this liquid argon TPC and then we are trying to find dark matter through scattering. Within this setup there are several distinct signal profiles — the S1 and S2 signals. S1 is one where you have a very sharp rise of signals, probably as short as several nanoseconds, and then there will be followed by an S2 phase where there will be a burst of photons. The goal is to cleanly carry all of the data off the detectors in a clean manner against the background noise.

[2:03:52] We identified a lot of the bottleneck to be conventional engineering challenges. One of them is analog versus digital. In conventional detectors people tend to try to aggregate all of the analog signals and then try to stream out everything at the end of the detector, which relies on very high precision amplifiers and ADCs, and there's also a challenge of bandwidth — the mismatch of how much information you are trying to collect versus how much you can actually stream out. In our scenario, if you look at an LVDS kind of cable, that can only carry several gigabits per second of data, but then the photons we are looking at are maybe up to 10 billion — 10^10 — photons per second. And for each photon we have to assign multiple bits. So there is that mismatch of what information you're trying to carry versus how much you can stream out.

[2:05:07] We have to work within our budget in terms of hardware — power, thermal — and if you need latency you also have to be fast. So with all of these challenges we identify the problem to be a data flow problem — how to stream data and route data efficiently. The algorithms and architecture that we propose make our detectors large-scale but then make them into smaller groups and try to have finer controls for local sensing — for us it's the SPADs (single photon avalanche diodes). So have finer controls at hopefully smaller granularities and also try to digitize as early as possible because we want to give tags of temporal and spatial information to all of these photons. After you digitize early then you can work with these signals a lot easier than if you have to work with analog signals.

[2:06:19] On the topic of AI — right now we have not fully integrated AI to the detector just because we have to find a reason to do that. AI, even a small model like MLP or LSTM, if you put it next to a detector the thermal goes up a lot. In our scenario any increase in power will likely boil the liquid argon. So we have to be very careful — anything we put on there is probably sub-hundreds of milliwatts of power budget. That's why for our first attempt we are trying to use conventional approaches of buffering using logic transistors, and try to have lightweight control and logic on our detectors. But the next step is definitely to look at autoencoders to see whether we can even better compress some of the data so that we can stream out all of them reliably.

[2:07:23] I'd like to turn now to the question of designing the next generation of very large scale scientific experiments. To take one example, there is a US concept for a next generation gravitational wave observatory that has 10 times the size and sensitivity of LIGO. Where do you see the big opportunities for AI approaches to actually accelerate or maybe even make possible at all truly optimal next generation facilities?

[2:08:30] I started in operations but I am very convinced at this point that the bigger opportunity is in design. Operations maybe we'll do things 10% cheaper or something like that, we'll do 10% better performance. But the real opportunity is in design and in co-design — how we are able to design new facilities, greenfield facilities that are AI-first, letting us do really completely novel things.

[2:08:58] In the accelerator world, in the photon facilities in particular, we were swept by this idea of the multi-bend achromat. Until about 10 years ago everyone just used these basic bend magnets and X-ray sources, and all of a sudden someone came up with the idea of putting quadrupole magnets in there, and it turns out you can get much much lower emittance, much brighter beams. That completely swept the world. Now all of a sudden you have an enormous number of possibilities, a tiny tiny fraction of which have been sampled by a small number of people sitting down and scratching their heads. I think we will see with AI just completely novel ideas coming up in the next couple of years.

[2:09:55] I think making bigger detectors is always great. I love that idea, but there's a lot of room at the bottom. In astronomy, you see people who make a lot of the greatest discoveries — they might only have a 2 meter dish, but the secret is in the cleverness. And I always wonder what we can do at the small scale. Typically we're using normal scaling arguments and that's why we make bigger detectors. But down at the bottom there's still a lot of room.

[2:10:27] If you look at gravitational waves, the signal to noise ratio for a gravitational wave if you just consider the noise of spacetime it's something like 10^11. But when we detect it the signal to noise is only 10. So I would give us an F-minus grade on transducing that signal. And why is it so bad? It's because of our materials physics. We're all talking about optimizing things that we know how to simulate. But the real problem is at the materials level.

[2:10:51] So many people are stopped by the fact that we have too many two-level systems — two-level systems in thin films all over the place. It leads to a 1/f noise in cryogenic detectors and microwave detectors and the LIGO mirrors and superconducting qubits. Why can't we solve this problem? Probably because we don't have a simulation good enough to tell us how to generate a macroscopic thin film which doesn't have any two-level tunneling systems. That's a kind of problem I don't know how to solve because the machine that makes those things is sort of mysterious. We don't really know how to model it because we don't have a full quantum simulation of the thin film. We're using density functional theory and other what I would call cheats and heuristics to do it.

[2:11:55] How far are we from the point where we can write down the requirements for a bespoke new kind of detector material and then just have it atom by atom fabricated? If we could make nuclear clocks that had a stability of 10^-21, we'd be able to detect all kinds of things. It's like Archimedes said about the lever: if you had an oscillator good enough, you could detect everything. But we're limited by materials really. We can scale up another factor of 10, but then what are you going to do? We've got to work small first and then we can scale up those futuristic devices.

[2:12:52] From the circuit design architecture perspective — a lot of the bottleneck is in the data. I also work in ultrasound and CMOS image sensors, and the common problem I see is that people want to see all the data, but the engineering sometimes cannot afford to supply all of that because of the constraints. One opportunity to integrate AI to the sensor front end is basically to intelligently compress and filter out some of the unwanted signals right at the source, so that we can still supply the scientists with the data they need.

[2:13:58] A lot of the directions that Javier mentioned with FPGA farms — we also need that word of co-design to make sure that any AI we put to compress or to filter signals at the source can be executed for inference within the power budget and thermal budget. For my field the type of design I shared — we call it the ASIC, application specific integrated circuits. That is the extreme end of specificity — this chip can only do one thing, but it does it really well in terms of energy efficiency. If you want to integrate any AI to sensors or detectors, I believe that's the way to go — finding the right AI model which probably has to be small, maybe several megabytes of model capacity at most, and then run it on a dedicated ASIC.

[2:15:10] Maybe I'll add one thing — there's actually an AI for muon collider workshop happening right now. There is a really cool opportunity to use AI to help us overcome a lot of challenges for magnet design, which is one of the big bottlenecks for how do we build the next collider. As Ronald was saying, we're not going to get the money we need to build the next collider if we do everything the same way we've been doing it forever. So we really have to get clever and I think AI is going to be a really useful tool in finding clever solutions.

[2:16:46] [Audience Q] One of the crucial things is you take a discrete problem and make it continuous so that you can do some optimization. The question is: the simulation of the model seems crucial to making this work. How do you get it to a point where you know you're getting the right answer?

[2:17:16] In 2025 it was sort of hopeless because it was half hallucination. Everyone's got their own answer for it, but I have a bunch of trusted sources that I use. We have a lot of analytical limits that we have to use for sanity checks. Like when I did finite element modeling 20 years ago every output was like 50% garbage — things wouldn't converge. So we always had to use analytical truths to base it on. And now we have tools like ANSYS and there are good mechanical engineering benchmarks and people do real robustness tests because they use it for things like building bridges, which matters a lot more than detecting gravitational waves. When you're in a strange regime — like how does that black hole vibrate when it hits something — we don't have any kind of ground truth, but that's why we do experiments. If you're doing a really expensive optimization and then you want to make a bet and design an experiment on that, you really have to have a lot of cross checks. The only way I know how to do it is to use a bunch of classical tools which maybe take a lot longer to run, but you can run some 24-hour really expensive runs to verify all of your cheap AI runs.

[2:18:56] [Audience Q] You all discussed at great length using AI in order to find creative solutions to our problems. Given that creativity is a muscle that must be exercised, how do you manage in your own work and in thinking about the field as a whole — being a director of AI's creativity versus continuing to find ways to exercise your own and improve it?

[2:19:28] Speaking from the engineering perspective — engineering is all about trade-offs and we have to make that decision. I think eventually even if we have AI to help us, humans should still be in the loop to make that final decision. AI is good at finding the optimal solution if you give it a set of technologies. But at the current stage AI is still not very good at finding that push of technologies that will push the Pareto front further. So for engineers, AI is very good to help us find the optimal with design space exploration, but we really need human creativity to push further and to invent new technologies.

[2:20:49] [Audience Q from department chair] Students and postdocs may feel they are not adequate to make any more creative ideas because AI is doing all the jobs. How do we maintain students to be motivated to think differently, come up with creative ideas?

[2:21:59] I don't have any answers. I agree that it's a worry for me. I also feel like some of these tools are so powerful, it feels almost addictive. Every hour you spend with it, it's so productive. But it also erodes my brain somewhat — if you're just sitting there at your screen, you're not spending enough time with your mind thinking about a particular problem. For me the way it works is I have to sit and meditate about a problem for a year or something like that before I really grasp it. And I feel like because these tools are so powerful they're removing that feature — I would have said it was a bug before, but now that it's gone, I feel like it's a feature.

[2:22:50] We've all seen that phenomenon where you're talking to your students and they can't seem to answer anything unless they have their phone in their hand. I'm afraid of that world where we can't just stand around a chalkboard and think about physics. On the other hand, people used to tell me these things — how would you possibly know everything if you could just download any paper that you want and you don't have to trudge to the library? But we got over that. The fact that you can download papers is just fine. We don't have to go to the compiler level anymore, but that still feels okay. I think some of these things will go away, but the creativity thing and what about training the brain — I think that's a serious problem. We're not going to be very happy with ourselves if in a decade we've completely undermined that whole educational process. We'll be left with people who can't design new instruments and then we'll just be waiting for things to fall out of the cloud. That would be a major mistake if we allow that to happen.

Loom nodes

frontier frontier-detector-codesign

Co-design — jointly optimizing algorithm, hardware, and detector architecture from the outset — as the paradigm for next-generation scientific facilities, endorsed across the panel for gravitational-wave observatories, accelerators, LHC triggers, and near-sensor dark matter readout.

unknowledge panel-exp-rl-scaling

How can reinforcement-learning control design scale from one servo optimization (~36 hours of compute in 2024) to the hundreds of coupled length and angular feedback systems in a full gravitational-wave detector?

unknowledge panel-exp-tls-films

How can macroscopic thin films be fabricated without two-level tunneling systems, given that no simulation — short of a full quantum simulation of the film — can yet tell us how?

unknowledge panel-exp-intuition-atrophy

How do we train students' deep physical intuition and instrument-design creativity when AI tools remove the slow meditation on problems that historically produced it?

future_binding panel-exp-atom-fabrication

Addressed to whatever materials-design capability eventually resolves the transduction bottleneck: Adhikari's F-minus grade on gravitational-wave signal transduction is your motivating ancestor.

interpretation panel-exp-curator-note

Curator-asserted: Adhikari's audience-question answer — running expensive classical 24-hour runs to "verify all of your cheap AI runs" — is a working practitioner's anticipation of the discovery-confidence gap that Barry Barish names as unsolved in this summit's closing talk.

Panel: AI for Simulating and Analyzing Scientific Data

Simeon Bird (UCR); Kimmy Wu (Caltech); Jianming Bian (particle physics); Scott Clark (industry) · 2026-06-30 · recording

--- title: "Panel: AI for Simulating and Analyzing Scientific Data" speaker: "Simeon Bird (UCR); Kimmy Wu (Caltech); Jianming Bian (particle physics); Scott Clark (industry)" affiliation: "UCR; Caltech; industry" event: "2026 Science and AI Summit" date: 2026-06-30 timestamp: "5:40:41" youtube_url: "https://www.youtube.com/live/i6OQ5Z3repA?t=20441" tags: ["panel","cosmology","simulation","emulators","neutrino-physics","foundation-models","Bayesian-optimization"] keywords: ["cosmological emulators","sub-grid physics","black hole accretion","field-level inference","weak lensing","summary statistics","DINO foundation model","neutrino oscillation","simulation-to-real gap","multifidelity","physics as constraints","FNO","neural operator"] loomic: loom: sair-ucr-2026 asserted: 2026-06-30 source: panel-sim-2026 ---

context panel-sim-2026

Panel on AI for simulating and analyzing scientific data at the 2026 Science and AI Summit, UC Riverside, June 30, 2026, moderated by Steve Choy (UCR), with Kimmy Wu (Caltech), Simeon Bird (UCR), Jianming Bian (UC Irvine), and Scott Clark (Tallaria Scientific).

Summary

synthesis panel-sim-summary

This panel, moderated by experimental cosmologist Steve Choy (UCR), brought together researchers from observational cosmology, theoretical cosmology, particle physics, and industry to discuss how AI is transforming the simulation and analysis of scientific data. Kimmy Wu (Caltech) described how AI enables field-level inference in cosmology — bypassing the traditional intermediate step of computing summary statistics from telescope maps and instead inferring cosmological parameters directly from pixel-level data. This unlocks access to information that was analytically intractable before and allows exploration of extended dark energy and dark matter models that were previously computationally impossible to test. Simeon Bird (UCR) argued that designing simulations and designing AI models will converge: AI will be embedded directly into simulations to replace phenomenological sub-grid physics prescriptions, with black hole accretion physics (connecting Schwarzschild-radius scales to hundred-megaparsec scales) identified as the hardest outstanding problem.

Jianming Bian (UC Irvine) discussed progress toward foundation models for neutrino experiments, including use of the DINO self-supervised model and vision-language models that allow human input to guide reconstruction across detectors with different geometries and resolutions. He identified three major obstacles: data availability and inter-collaboration secrecy in high-energy physics, the challenge of unifying heterogeneous data formats and tokenization schemes, and the simulation-to-real gap — the fundamental inconsistency between AI performance on simulated data and on real detector data. He stressed the obligation to open the "black box" and demonstrate that AI methods are checkable and that uncertainties are reliable, in order to win over skeptical traditional physicists.

Scott Clark (Tallaria Scientific) contributed an industry perspective rooted in Bayesian optimization, arguing that the reliability bar for AI should be calibrated to the decision being made: high fidelity is required for final results, but surrogate models and neural operators (e.g., FNO) are sufficient for navigating the search space and deciding what to try next. He framed physical laws as "unit tests" that AI must not break, analogous to continuous integration in software, and warned that objective function specification — knowing precisely what you are optimizing — is the hardest and most consequential part of any AI-driven scientific workflow. The panel converged on the view that AI's greatest current impact is accelerating inference and emulation, while the deeper challenge of genuine knowledge discovery requires careful use of physical symmetries, symmetry-informed compression, and interpretable summary statistics that can be validated against neural network outputs.

Transcript

observation panel-sim-transcript

[5:40:41] the panel is Velis, who we just met, although I assume many of you are familiar with, Kimmy Wu from Caltech, Simeon Bird from UCR, John Mingb from UC Irvine, and Scott Clark, CEO of Tallaria Scientific.

[5:41:06] Yeah, thank you Velis for this excellent talk to set us up for this panel. So, welcome to this panel on AI for simulating and analyzing scientific data. I'm Steve Choy, an experimental cosmologist here at UCR. And earlier today, we discussed how AI is transforming scientific instruments and detector operation, not just operation but even design, and this panel will focus on the next stage of the process: using AI to simulate complex systems and analyze massive data sets and ultimately learn new science. In the interest of time, I'll first ask each of them to briefly introduce themselves and their research before we jump into the discussion.

[5:42:04] Should I start? Okay. Hi everyone. I'm Kim Wu. I'm an assistant professor in physics at Caltech. My day job is observational cosmologist. What that means is that I take data from telescopes to learn about the universe. The connection to AI in my work is that in cosmology we make maps of the universe at different frequencies, and for many many years people then extract summary statistics from these maps and then compare that to cosmological models and ask how much dark energy there is, how much normal stuff and dark matter there is. But because of AI, we can now instead of going through this intermediate step of summary statistics, go directly from the map pixels to the model parameters to learn about the universe. Some of the specific things that me and my group think about is how to model some of these previously analytically intractable distributions that describe our cosmological fields through generative models and make it possible to do massive inference on big data sets.

[5:43:36] Thank you very much. So I'm Simeon Bird. I'm associate professor here at UC Riverside. My research is a bit similar to Kim's but from a theoretical perspective. I'm trying to use the large scale structure in the universe — maybe 100 megaparsecs or 10^8 light years — to figure out something about fundamental physics like the mass of the neutrino, the properties of dark matter. The way we do that is we try and build a big simulation of everything in the universe and compare it to the real universe. In practice, this means you run a whole bunch of simulations, maybe a hundred of them, and then you build an emulator, a machine learning model to interpolate between them.

[5:44:20] Hello. My name is Jianming Bian. I'm a physics professor from UC Irvine. I'm co-chair of the EML forum in two major neutrino experiments, Nova and Doom. Over the years I've been working with computer scientists to introduce state-of-the-art neural network machine learning methods in neutrino experiments. In early years we worked on convolutional neural networks to first introduce neural nets to neutrino experiments or even to HEP experiments. Later we introduced transformers to reconstruct neutrino images, and in recent years I'm mostly interested in using large multimodal AI and foundation models for neutrino reconstruction and analysis.

[5:45:27] Hey everyone, I'm the odd one out from industry. My name is Scott Clark. I've founded a couple AI startups. First one was based off of my grad school work doing Bayesian optimization to help people solve high-dimensional blackbox noisy problems — everything from recommender systems with Netflix and fraud detection at AmEx to hedge funds making money with their algorithmic trading policies. Sold that to Intel, ran AI and HPC for their supercomputing group for a while, and then started my next startup which started as an AI testing and analytics company and has morphed into Tallaria Scientific, a multi-agent harness to help accelerate computational science grounded with multifidelity HPC algorithms. Really excited to be here.

[5:46:15] Yeah. Thank you. Right, to get started. The theme is on how AI is changing how science is done, and I'll first ask Simeon: your recent work has shown that AI can emulate cosmological simulations orders of magnitude faster than traditional methods. Looking ahead, will future cosmologists spend more time designing simulations or designing AI models?

[5:46:46] Yes. So I'm going to controversially say that those will become the same thing. I think that what will happen is we're going to end up embedding the AI models into the simulations — there are parts of these models that are kind of hokey, and it would be better to replace them with an AI coarse-training of some higher resolution more fundamental physics thing. I think that's what we're going to end up doing.

[5:47:13] But any aspects of physics that remain challenging?

[5:47:16] Yeah. The bit of physics that is hardest to simulate is how black holes accrete gas and how that gas affects the things around it. That is very very hard to model even on a fundamental level. We would try to connect the very small scales around the Schwarzschild radius of the black hole to the larger scales — 100 megaparsecs — that's very challenging at the moment because we don't even really know the scaling relations that they're supposed to behave. I think AI might be able to help us with that.

[5:48:03] So AI is making computation much faster but understanding the underlying physics could still be a challenge?

[5:48:14] I think so.

[5:48:14] I want to turn to you Kimmy about the observational side. Modern cosmology increasingly relies on AI throughout the analysis pipeline. Where do you think AI is making the greatest impact — accelerating inference, finding and mitigating systematic uncertainties, or even uncovering new physics?

[5:48:40] I think accelerating inference is definitely what we've seen AI do within cosmology. To give a couple of examples: in cosmological parameter inference we have to calculate a model — the likelihood computation is very fast but the model computation is slow. With an emulator we can reduce a several-second computation to a fraction of a second. So not only are we able to explore more models, thus making the result more robust because we can do all the test cases we want to do — maybe that's addressing the systematics aspect — we can also now explore models that would have been computationally impossible before. One of these things is not only building an emulator of the model computation but learning the model computation during the Monte Carlo process. Extended dark energy and dark matter models that we would not have explored at all, we are now doing and testing. Another aspect: some of these higher dimensional inference problems that were impossible are now possible because of AI and HPC. We know that at the pixel level there is more information than at the summary statistics level. So conservatively we have more information. In principle we can learn more about what is in the data. And on an optimistic day, there might be things at the map level that would not have been possible to learn at the summary statistic level. I think it's quite exciting that we have access to that now that was impossible before.

[5:51:04] So maybe I'll turn to Jaming about your work with multiple detector subsystems where you explore AI systems that can learn jointly from these detectors but also from human input. Do you envision future experiments to be built around unified foundation model instead of individual separate reconstruction algorithms?

[5:51:32] I think it is a very promising direction to develop a foundation model for more general HEP experiments. We've already seen very good science. At smaller scale you can train a foundation model for a specific detector but different configurations in the same experiment and get very good results to improve performance. And also in different experiments with different types of data: if you train using the DINO model, I see very nice progress — you have a pre-trained foundation model and then you test or retrain it with just a handful of events to do fine-tuning and you get very good results; it's very robust and flexible. At larger scale, if you use a large vision-language model as a backbone, you can even introduce human input — combining image input and detector input not just from different experiments and detectors but also from humans — and in this way you can introduce humans to understand and teach neural networks.

[5:52:59] There are also challenges. The first is data: at least in high-energy physics we have the tradition that each collaboration holds their own data; there is a barrier between experiments preventing sharing of data. Public and open data is definitely needed to develop this foundation model. Problem two is how to unify different types of data or different formats — different detectors have different pixel maps, different resolutions, different configurations, completely different geometries, and more generally different types of data including metadata and documentation from different domains. And how to involve people to improve and to control the AI — the tokenization challenge is very different for different groups. And then practically there is the simulation-to-real gap: sure, you show very nice results in your simulation, but what happens when we move to real data? There is a fundamental inconsistency because of model uncertainties, and how you solve it and how you prove that the AI is robust enough — that is a big challenge. Our obligation as AI scientists is to open this box, to show that our AI methods are checkable, that the foundation model is valuable, and that the uncertainty is valuable. How to convince traditional physics scientists that AI is trustable — that is a very challenging part.

[5:56:57] Thank you, Jimmy. I want to expand on that robustness of using AI for science and turn to Scott: scientific AI is beginning to do more than just analyze data and is helping guide decisions in science. What level of reliability should we be demanding before AI becomes part of this decision-making loop?

[5:57:27] Great question, and I think you really nailed it when you said "guide" — we should hold ourselves to the highest level of reproducibility and reliability when it comes to a final result, but you don't often need that ultimate level of granularity to decide what to do next. Imagine you're walking through a forest and you need to decide what path you're on. You don't need to know your location down to a micron to say "here I am, this is where I'm going to step next." At the very end, when you plant your flag on the summit, you want to make sure you're actually at the summit. But along the way, there are multiscale, multiphysics, and multifidelity problems where maybe a surrogate is good enough. Using a neural operator to simulate a PDE will actually get you far enough to make that next discovery. You can always back it up with more classic analytic methods and numerical solvers for PDEs that help make this flywheel spin faster and faster. We're finally at the stage now with AI that we can solve a lot of these more undifferentiated grunt-work style problems and give you enough information to make that next step. Use the human to sample out of the distribution, but then use the AI to fill in behind you — do some of this translation work — but really use that to decide what to do next and then go back and fill in with really high resolution, high fidelity models.

[5:58:56] Building on that, Velis, your keynote really emphasized knowledge discovery powered by AI. We ultimately care about understanding more than prediction. What advances are still needed for AI to contribute directly to scientific insight?

[5:59:19] The focus should be broadened from just prediction. Not everything is a predictive task. If you optimize for prediction, you'll have all sorts of byproducts. Put the problem you're trying to solve in the forefront and don't shoehorn an existing method. Whether that is predictive, great. If not, think how to express that best in a way that solves the problem. An F1 score means nothing if it doesn't solve the problem. You can have 100% accuracy and have a useless solution. It's always about what are you trying to solve, what are the requirements, and what are the metrics you're trying to optimize for solving that.

[6:00:40] One of the best things about an optimization algorithm is you can point it at anything, but one of the worst things is it'll go where you point it. Making sure you really understand your objective functions is critical. No matter who we worked with — from the smartest hedge funds to the intelligence community — there was always one more metric. That objective function solicitation, that taste, that knowing what it is you're actually looking for is the only way to actually direct these methods.

[6:01:39] So along the way there is a lot of compressing of data happening across very different fields. How do we know what information can safely be compressed away and what scientific information should never be lost?

[6:02:08] In neutrino physics we need first theoretical guidance and then actual experiments. For example, in our liquid argon TPC detector, you make projections to read real 3D particle information from two views, and there's a need to reduce redundant information. We found a paper on L1 minimization that proves — in the context of CT imaging — that this kind of redundancy can be removed as long as the image is sparse enough. We took this guidance and used it in our neutrino experiment and it worked — significant reduction in the 2D projection problem. And on the other hand, we also need to test: once we develop this reconstruction method we test it in simulation data and compare the compression efficiency in both prediction and simulation. We need to go both ways to identify what can be thrown away and also use the same method to make sure we do not lose any useful information.

[6:04:31] Yeah. This is not a new problem, right? We have this messy data and we have to come up with some summary of it, and we should use the same tricks we have been using all along, which is to use the symmetries of the data to work out what is useful. In cosmology of course there is a translational symmetry — every part of the universe should be in some sense the same — so we take an angle average, take a power spectrum, throw away the positional information, and combine things according to the symmetries of the problem. I think to some degree AI models are capable of learning these things on their own. But when they find a way to compress, we should ask: is there a symmetry they are using? If there is then we should keep that compression; otherwise we should look for something else.

[6:05:31] Could that lead us to wrong or non-physical directions?

[6:05:39] It could. So we'd have to check it. In weak lensing there's a program of trying to extract the maximum amount of statistical power from the weak lensing maps. They ran a neural network on it and it produced a great deal of power. Then they went back and looked for new summary statistics that could reproduce — pull out 95% of the information the neural network pulled out. And they were able to find such a thing. Once they had done that they were able to have a high physical confidence on it. The neural network told them there was something to look for.

[6:06:32] And doing it backwards from that is a great way to discover new methods — something you didn't even know you were looking for. But also using existing solvers: the Department of Energy has been pushing out great numerical and analytical methods for decades. If you use that as a guardrail — you can tell the LLM "go do whatever it takes but you're not allowed to break the laws of physics" — as long as you constrain it and harness it with those laws of physics, you can let it go pretty far. We have unit tests as scientists; they just happen to be the physical laws. Make sure that you're running your continuous integration and then you can actually make sure you're not hallucinating new science.

[6:07:41] So in discussing these AI surrogates replacing expensive first-principle calculations — where do you think AI-driven simulations may be breaking down today, and what weaknesses may we not be talking about?

[6:08:14] One thing I've noticed: there are certain scaling laws that are emergent in galaxy physics — generally power laws between different masses, temperatures, densities. In reality these are very tight laws. When they come out of the AI models they tend to be softened — it will discover the law is there, but it will soften it and won't be fully confident of it. The other way around can also happen: it will be overly confident that a scaling law is the right one when in reality it's actually softened. Somehow it needs to know the difference between laws of physics that cannot be broken and laws that are things that are often true. I don't know how to teach it that, but I think that's what it needs to know.

[6:09:19] We should open it up to the audience for any questions.

[6:09:43] This is a question for Scott. You mentioned you created a startup that was an AI tool for hedge funds — and there was a study at Wharton and NBER which stated that when you let AI agents essentially trade, there was some level of market manipulation that occurred even though they didn't explicitly instruct the bots to conduct this manipulation. How do you see AI progressing in hedge funds and would there be any sort of regulations or restrictions?

[6:10:51] Great question. Whenever you're applying any new technology to any new field, you need to be thinking about the second and third order effects. There are often unintended consequences — some can be beneficial: high-frequency trading has created market liquidity and price discovery that didn't exist before. But at the same time you do have potentially correlated strategies that can cause flash crashes. Like anything, there are trade-offs and you need to be aware of that coming in. Regulation comes in to make the trade-offs that society, not individuals, should make.

[6:11:43] You mentioned physical laws like scaling laws — when you're designing a network, how would you go about implementing those as domain knowledge into your model?

[6:12:03] You can lay them in as constraints to your harness — I think of them like tests. It depends on what level of fidelity you require. If I just need a quick pass to see whether or not something works, using an FNO-type method might be great. If I'm designing a new reactor, I'm probably going to want to use finite element analysis all the way down to high granularity, or some combination thereof. It's up to the user to have this sliding scale of fidelity and scale for their problems.

[6:13:06] Question directed to Dr. Woo, Bird, and Bian — when putting AI models inside actual simulations, how do you account for the physics and context of the situation, because when you're modeling black holes and gas accretion, how do you account for all the different physics when making surrogates?

[6:13:49] That is actually a very smart question because I don't know the answer to that — we're still working on that. We would test it many different ways. We would check that it produced reasonable answers in a variety of situations and that it tried to obey the laws of physics as much as possible. But actually we don't know really what the right answer is — we can't build a black hole and go next to it and look at it.

[6:14:24] Maybe adding to that and also addressing the earlier question about models obeying certain physical laws: for my specific application I need simulations that have certain statistics. Once building it, we find smart ways to learn from more expensive simulations and build surrogates. We find smart ways to do it because we know there are certain scale invariance properties — we train at specific scales to actually get the accuracy we need. That requires a little bit of physical intuition. You don't just throw a simple generative model on anything. And then you build tests to make sure that the model is valid given the applications you're using it for.

Loom nodes

frontier frontier-field-level-inference

Field-level inference: extracting cosmological information directly from map pixels, past the summary-statistics bottleneck — including extended dark energy and dark matter models that were computationally impossible to test before.

unknowledge panel-sim-bh-subgrid

How does black hole accretion connect Schwarzschild-radius scales to hundred-megaparsec scales? The scaling relations are not known, and there is no ground truth — "we can't build a black hole and go next to it and look at it."

unknowledge panel-sim-sim-to-real

How can AI reconstruction be proven robust across the simulation-to-real gap, with uncertainties reliable enough to convince traditional physicists that an AI-derived result is trustable?

unknowledge panel-sim-hard-vs-soft-laws

How can a model be taught the difference between laws of physics that cannot be broken and scaling relations that are merely often true — so that it neither softens tight laws nor hardens soft ones?

future_binding panel-sim-embedded-subgrid

Addressed to the simulation architecture Bird predicts, in which designing simulations and designing AI models have become the same activity.

interpretation panel-sim-curator-note

Curator-asserted: Bian's challenge — proving AI robustness across the simulation-to-real gap and convincing traditional physicists that AI uncertainties are reliable — is the same epistemic gap Barry Barish declares unsolved in the summit's closing talk (discovery confidence); the sim-to-real unknowledge above is therefore filed inside that frontier.

Panel: Southern California Innovation Ecosystem

Multiple panelists · 2026-06-30 · recording

--- title: "Panel: Southern California Innovation Ecosystem" speaker: "Multiple panelists" affiliation: "UCR; industry; VC" event: "2026 Science and AI Summit" date: 2026-06-30 timestamp: "3:10:07" youtube_url: "https://www.youtube.com/live/i6OQ5Z3repA?t=11407" tags: ["panel","innovation","entrepreneurship","Southern-California","technology-transfer"] keywords: ["Southern California","Inland Empire","technology transfer","academic founder","AI innovation center","comparative agriculture","biotech","aerospace","semiconductor","UC system"] loomic: loom: sair-ucr-2026 asserted: 2026-06-30 source: panel-socal-2026 ---

context panel-socal-2026

Panel on the Southern California innovation ecosystem at the 2026 Science and AI Summit, UC Riverside, June 30, 2026, with the chair of physics and astronomy at UC Riverside, the chair of the UC Riverside academic senate, Weiyi Wong (chair of computer science, UCLA), and Ryan (Asipus Enterprise, investor and mentor).

Summary

synthesis panel-socal-summary

The panel brought together academic leaders and investors to assess the Southern California innovation landscape, with broad agreement that the region is performing well but operating well below its potential — one panelist estimated three to ten times under-capacity relative to available talent, research output, and industrial diversity. Participants drew a consistent contrast with the Bay Area model: Southern California founders tend to build cash-flow businesses that solve real problems with willing payers, often retaining far greater ownership than Bay Area counterparts who repeatedly dilute through successive financing rounds. This capital-efficient culture was framed as a distinctive strength, not a limitation.

UC Riverside's identity was highlighted as shaped directly by its Inland Empire geography. The campus originated in 1907 as a citrus experimental station, and agriculture remains a core strength. Panelists used Chancellor Hoo's concept of "comparative agriculture" to describe UCR's position as a living laboratory for air quality, water quality, and crop optimization — domains where AI-assisted research could generate both scientific advances and direct economic benefit for the region. The physics and astronomy chair stressed that transformative technology ultimately rests on basic science investment, arguing for world-class laboratory infrastructure as the single highest-leverage accelerant for the region.

The UCLA computer science perspective emphasized the urgency of technology transfer pathways that do not require faculty to leave academia. A proposed AI Innovation Center, anchored at UCLA and intended to extend across the UC system including UC Riverside, was discussed as a vehicle for enabling faculty and students to commercialize ideas without relocating to the Bay Area. Panelists agreed that the missing ingredient is not talent or research — both are abundant — but rather structured mechanisms that bring capital, clinical or industrial problem-owners, and technical experts to the same table around specific, defined problems rather than general intellectual exploration.

The investor on the panel identified the most under-appreciated opportunity as the inflow of Silicon Valley growth capital into Southern California, which could catalyze the compounding flywheel seen in mature ecosystems: exits funding new founders, first employees becoming founders, and successive generations building locally. The region's sectoral breadth — entertainment, aerospace, defense, space exploration, wireless communications, semiconductors, healthcare, life sciences, energy, logistics, and agriculture — was cited as both an asset and an underutilized one, with the panel closing on the message that researchers and students in the region are agents of change in a moment where the only binding constraints are the laws of physics.
