UCR Chancellor's Address: Excellence, Access, and Regional Mission

UCR Chancellor · 2026-06-30 · recording

--- title: "UCR Chancellor's Address: Excellence, Access, and Regional Mission" speaker: "UCR Chancellor" affiliation: "UC Riverside" event: "2026 Science and AI Summit" date: 2026-06-30 timestamp: "2:45:36" youtube_url: "https://www.youtube.com/live/i6OQ5Z3repA?t=9936" tags: ["UCR","equity","access","institutional","social-mobility"] keywords: ["UC Riverside","Hispanic Serving Institution","Pell Grant","first-generation students","social mobility","Oasis Innovation Park","AI institute","Inland Empire"] loomic: loom: sair-ucr-2026 asserted: 2026-06-30 source: chancellor-2026-address ---

context chancellor-2026-address

Address by the UCR Chancellor at the 2026 Science and AI Summit, UC Riverside, June 30, 2026, on institutional priorities and regional mission.

Summary

synthesis chancellor-address-summary

UC Riverside is the only research university in Inland Southern California, serving a region of five million people across Riverside and San Bernardino counties — a population that, if considered a state, would rank 25th in the nation. The region is one of America's fastest-growing and most diverse, serving as a major logistics corridor where Highways 215, 91, and 60 converge, and increasingly shaped by healthcare, biotechnology, advanced manufacturing, and clean transportation. Despite its economic significance, the region has historically received less investment in higher education, research, and innovation than other parts of California — a gap that UCR is positioned to close.

UCR holds a distinctive position as the only Association of American Universities (AAU) member institution that is simultaneously a Hispanic Serving Institution (HSI) and an Asian American, Native American, and Pacific Islander Serving Institution (AANAPISI). Its undergraduate student body is 37% Hispanic and 33% Asian Pacific Islander and Native American — together 70% of undergraduates. More than 40% receive Pell Grants, and close to half are first-generation college students, yet Pell Grant recipients graduate at essentially the same rate as non-recipients. For five of the last seven years, US News and World Report has ranked UC Riverside number one in social mobility. The Chancellor frames this not as a tension between excellence and access, but as proof that these goals reinforce each other — achieved at scale with 7,000–8,000 graduates per year.

The Chancellor outlined four strategic priorities: deepening commitment to student academic success and social mobility through transformative research university experiences; accelerating mission-driven research and entrepreneurship, including a campus-wide AI institute, a $6.2 million Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation, and the Oasis Innovation Park designed to move ideas from discovery to marketplace; expanding UCR Health with additional clinics and a specialty ambulatory clinic; and strengthening K-16 and industry partnerships to support college attainment, workforce development, and economic growth across the region.

Looking forward, the Chancellor cast UCR as a bridge connecting Northern and Southern California — linking Los Angeles to Inland Southern California and the Coachella Valley — and argued that while much of the technology and industry that transformed the world was built in Northern California, the next chapter of California's innovation will increasingly be written in the south. UCR aims to link talent, research, entrepreneurship, venture capital, and economic opportunity across these regions.

Transcript

observation chancellor-address-transcript

[2:37:54] I have the opportunity to explain UC Riverside and my priorities. I won't talk about AI and science, but I want to talk about our priorities.

[2:38:09] UC Riverside — we're one of the 10 campuses of the University of California, but we're the only research university in Inland Southern California.

[2:38:27] That's two counties — Riverside and San Bernardino. The two counties together have about five million people. If you look at population and consider the two counties as a state, we rank number 25 — right in the middle of the 50 states. Of course we're not a state; we're just a small region of California, but it's a big region.

[2:38:59] Five million people. This is one of America's fastest growing and most diverse regions in California and in the country.

[2:39:11] Much of the nation's supplies go through our region. If you drove here, you will know three highways all merge at the corner of our downtown — Highway 215, 91, and California 60. That's where our goods get moved around from the port in Los Angeles; they come here and then get redistributed.

[2:39:40] I told a joke last night — if you were in Michigan or the University of Georgia and ordered something from Amazon, it usually took two and a half days to get to you. But here, if you order something in the morning, you get it in the afternoon.

[2:40:00] The region is increasingly shaped by logistics, healthcare, biotechnology, advanced manufacturing, and clean transportation. Yet despite its economic significance, the region has historically received less investment in higher education, research, innovation, and healthcare than any other part of California. But that is changing, and UC Riverside is at the center of that transformation.

[2:40:39] UC Riverside is a member of the Association of American Universities — 71 leading research universities in the country and also Canada.

[2:40:54] As Chancellor, I get to sit together with leaders from other top institutions like Harvard, Stanford, Berkeley, Michigan, UCLA and so on, talking about things important to higher education — federal funding, support for students, free speech, and all those important things impacting all of us.

[2:41:23] But we're the only AAU institution that is simultaneously a Hispanic Serving Institution and also an Asian American, Native American, and Pacific Islander Serving Institution.

[2:41:40] Our undergraduate student body consists of 37% Hispanic Americans and 33% Asian Pacific Islander and Native Americans. The two groups together is 70% of our undergraduate students.

[2:42:02] More than 40% of our students receive Pell Grants, and close to half of our undergraduates are first generation. Yet our Pell Grant recipients and non-Pell recipients graduate at essentially the same rate.

[2:42:21] That combination — supporting students who are first-gen or Pell recipients while being in the AAU — is very, very rare.

[2:42:37] It reflects a simple but powerful truth: that excellence and access are not competing goals, but they can support each other.

[2:42:49] For the last five out of seven years, US News and World Report ranked UC Riverside number one in social mobility. What that means is students coming from an economically disadvantaged background graduate here — with our academic support, student life, university culture — and get great jobs. The salary compared with what they would make with only a high school degree is significant.

[2:43:27] So the story of UC Riverside is not a story of excellence or access. It's a story of UC Riverside achieving excellence and access at scale — graduating between 7,000 and 8,000 students each year, serving one of the most dynamic and diverse regions of our country.

[2:44:02] When I joined UC Riverside last year, I outlined several priorities that will continue to guide our trajectory.

[2:44:10] First, we're deepening our commitment to our students' academic success and social mobility. We want every student to experience the full power of a world-class research university — not only through classroom learning, but through transformative experiences that shape their lives and careers.

[2:44:35] Second, we're accelerating mission-driven research, innovation, and entrepreneurship. We're investing in faculty across different disciplines, strengthening interdisciplinary research, expanding research infrastructure, and elevating faculty distinction.

[2:44:55] We invested in a campus-wide AI institute that supports research, workforce development, and deployment of AI in all things we do.

[2:45:06] We are launching a Center for Entrepreneurship and Innovation, supported through a philanthropic gift of $6.2 million. The center will help students move ideas from the laboratories or classrooms to communities through ventures, startups, and new jobs.

[2:45:31] We're also creating the infrastructure to accelerate the translation from research into impact. The Oasis Innovation Park is designed to be more than a research park. We envision it as a destination where ideas move more rapidly from discovery to the marketplace — where innovation translates into economic and social impact. It will be a place where students, faculty, entrepreneurs, startups, established companies, government agencies, and communities partner together, work side by side to develop solutions and create opportunities.

[2:46:19] Third, we're expanding UCR Health to improve healthcare throughout our region. We're investing in additional clinics and developing a plan for a specialty ambulatory clinic.

[2:46:35] Finally, we're strengthening partnerships that will support increased college attainment, workforce development, economic growth, and regional investment. Industry partnerships are essential as we expand research, accelerate innovation, create internships, support capstone projects, and broaden career pathways. We look forward to working with companies represented here.

[2:47:05] We also just launched a K-16 engagement council to coordinate our work with schools, community colleges, and regional universities.

[2:47:18] I know many of you are from the Bay Area or have ties to that area. We all know that many of the technologies, companies, and industries that have transformed the world were built in Northern California.

[2:47:34] But I believe the next chapter of California's innovation will continue to be written there — but increasingly it will also be written in Southern California.

[2:47:46] We believe UC Riverside can serve as a bridge to connect the north and the south of California, to connect Los Angeles to Inland Southern California and the Coachella Valley — the Palm Desert area — linking talent, research, entrepreneurship, venture capital, and economic opportunities across those regions.

[2:48:09] I look forward to exploring ways to work together to make this a reality. Thanks again to everyone for being here.

UCR Welcome and SAIR Foundation Opening

UCR Chancellor Hoo; Chuck Ng; Barry Barish · 2026-06-30 · recording

--- title: "UCR Welcome and SAIR Foundation Opening" speaker: "UCR Chancellor Hoo; Chuck Ng; Barry Barish" affiliation: "UC Riverside; SAIR Foundation" event: "2026 Science and AI Summit" date: 2026-06-30 timestamp: "0:16:36" youtube_url: "https://www.youtube.com/live/i6OQ5Z3repA?t=996" tags: ["SAIR-Foundation","UCR","opening","institutional","AI-science-integration"] keywords: ["SAIR Foundation","UC Riverside","science and AI","instrumentation gap","RACE Institute","Southern California Oasis","master key metaphor"] loomic: loom: sair-ucr-2026 asserted: 2026-06-30 source: ucr-welcome-2026 ---

context ucr-welcome-2026

Opening session of the 2026 Science and AI Summit, UC Riverside, June 30, 2026: welcome by UCR Chancellor Hoo, followed by SAIR Foundation co-founders Chuck Ng and Barry Barish.

Summary

synthesis ucr-welcome-summary

UCR Chancellor Hoo opened the 2026 Science and AI Summit by welcoming participants and acknowledging co-organizers: the Center for Experimental Cosmology and Instrumentation at UC Riverside and the Global Foundation for Science and AI Research (SAIR Foundation). He highlighted UCR's recently founded RACE Institute (Riverside Artificial Intelligence Research and Education), which unites over 85 interdisciplinary faculty across all seven colleges to advance machine learning and develop ethical, transparent AI solutions. The chancellor emphasized that UCR, as the only R1 research university in inland Southern California, occupies a distinctive position where technology, agricultural legacy, climate pressures, and global logistics intersect.

A central theme of the chancellor's remarks was the relationship between fundamental and applied research. Using a "master key" metaphor, he argued that translational research produces keys that open particular doors, while fundamental, curiosity-driven science produces a master key capable of opening many doors — including doors not yet known to exist. He stressed that foundational inquiry into physics, mathematics, and the nature of intelligence is the necessary bedrock for building robust next-generation AI systems.

Chuck Ng, co-founder of SAIR Foundation, framed the summit around SIR's founding conviction: that science and AI must be brought into the same room with equal seriousness to work on the same problems. He outlined three programmatic tracks for the day — AI's role in designing and operating scientific detectors, AI for simulating and analyzing scientific data at previously impossible scales, and AI as a tool for theory-building that changes how science thinks, not merely computes. He also noted Southern California and the Inland Empire as an underappreciated concentration of scientific talent and emerging technology.

Nobel laureate Barry Barish, co-founder of SAIR Foundation and part-time faculty at UC Riverside since 2018, described the institutional gap he identified in the university system: universities hire physicists to analyze data and publish results, but systematically underinvest in instrumentation expertise and laboratory technology development. The fields he highlighted — dark matter searches, cosmic microwave background studies, next-generation gravitational wave detectors — sit between physics and astronomy departments and require precisely the kind of deep technical infrastructure that universities tend not to build. The CECI group at UCR represents a deliberate effort to close that gap, and the SAIR Foundation's mission is to integrate AI into that instrumentation and analysis work.

[0:26:33] Barry Barish: First, welcome everybody from the host institution UCR, which you just heard. I'm going to tell you just a few minutes about the co-sponsor — a little bit about that, which is on the physics side.

[0:26:52] I'll just reflect for a minute. Chuck I've known for quite a while, and when this foundation was being formed it seemed to me that having a kind of marriage of a foundation that was working on the problems that can help us optimize how to take this growing field of AI and apply it to scientific problems — in our case physical sciences — was a really good thing to do, and to do it in a way that we work together in a foundation to create and support the most effective things seemed like the way to go.

[0:27:31] I've been part-time here at Riverside since 2018, and in that time we've created a new effort here which is working in the area between physics and astronomy. When I came, I was asked to look at the physics department and tell them what they might do to make it better. I identified this as an area that they should make appointments in and develop, for a couple of reasons.

[0:28:06] One is that there's both a physics and an astronomy department, yet physics and astronomy in most places are hard to bring together. Yet in science there's a whole stream of problems now that lie between physics and astronomy — we call parts of it cosmology, which is looking at the early universe. And then there's a whole series of problems: LIGO, like I worked on, which is somewhere in between physics and astronomy; looking for dark matter; exploring the sky looking at the cosmic microwave background — many areas in physics exploring these frontiers.

[0:29:01] These don't tend to fit into what is done in the big physics labs like Fermilab or SLAC, the big ones that have a lot of infrastructure, a lot of engineering. So it's left to physicists in the universities more or less to develop the technologies that we need to move forward in these fields — and they can be very technically ambitious, like LIGO or dark matter searches, which use the forefront of technologies including software, data analysis, and hopefully more and more AI to help us design the right instruments, make them work best, and analyze the data.

[0:29:47] So bringing these together is really something that we have worked toward here as we've developed a set of experiments. There's a meeting the next few days to talk about the technologies — and hopefully more and more we'll also integrate with the AI world, which is where I've tried to bring this merger together.

[0:30:33] As I said, this is done in universities. Universities tend to want to hire physicists that are doing the science — which means hiring the people that analyze the data and present a scientific result. And so there's been a big gap, I think, in universities developing the tools, the techniques, and the laboratory expertise that we need to explore this huge field of science that's becoming more and more important. So we've been developing that kind of effort technically here.
